reaches more families, <a href=\"http://on.app.com/2lCGEAA\">parents and guardians</a> face increasing pressure to talk to their kids about drugs, public health officials say.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>How they respond matters, according to a body of research from academic journals and\u00a0nonprofit organizations. But that research differs on how to respond. And not everyone agrees\u00a0that honesty is the only policy.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Some psychologists stress caution.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Angelo Valente, executive director of Partnership for a Drug-Free New Jersey, a drug prevention organization, said the question should be viewed as an opportunity.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\"The fact that your child is coming to you is extremely important,\" he said, siding strongly with parents being honest. If dishonesty comes to light, it can harm trust and that can keep a child from coming forward later, he said.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\"You want them to continue to be able to build trust,\" he said. \"You want the parent to be a resource during difficult times in adolescence.\"</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-PyqKl-3k_eA\" class=\"story-asset oembed-asset\"><div class=\"story-oembed-wrap \"><div \n        class=\"js-oembed story-oembed story-oembed-usa-today story-oembed-type-link\"\n        data-oembed-type=\"link\"\n        data-oembed-provider=\"usa-today\"><style>\n    .oembed-asset-link { border-bottom: 1px solid #e1e1e1; }\n    .oembed-link-anchor { display: block; clear: both; }\n    p.oembed-link-desc { font-size: 100%; color: #666; font-weight: normal; margin: 0 14px 14px 14px; font-family: 'Futura Today Light'; text-align: left; line-height: 120%; }\n</style><div class=\"oembed-asset oembed-asset-link oembed-asset-usa-today oembed-simple-link-container\"><a href=\"http://www.usatoday.com/story/news/nation-now/2017/02/24/deadly-drug-overdoses-more-than-doubled-since-1999/98345458/\" data-track-label=\"newrecircinline\" class=\"oembed-simple-link oembed-link-anchor\"><p class=\"oembed-link-desc\">Deadly drug overdoses more than doubled since 1999</p><div style=\"clear: both;\"></div></a></div></div></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Along with any disclosure of past drug use, parents should make sure their children know that drugs \u2014\u00a0from opioids to synthetic drugs like bath salts \u2014\u00a0are far more dangerous than most drugs\u00a0were 20 or 30 years ago, Valente said.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The national organization, Partnership for Drug-Free Kids, provides advice on how to answer the question\u00a0in a\u00a0<a href=\"https://www.drugfree.org/wp-content/uploads/2014/05/How-to-talk-to-your-kids-about-drugs-if-you-did-drugs.pdf\">tip sheet</a>\u00a0and a more detailed\u00a0<a href=\"http://drugfree.org/MJTalkKit/\">kit\u00a0</a>specifically about marijuana.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\"Kids want to have that open dialogue,\" said Kristi Rowe, a spokeswoman for the group. \"That doesn't mean you have to give them all the detail in technicolor.\"</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>One of the group's broader points: the right approach depends on your child.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Telling the truth, for instance, carries its own pitfalls, according to one prominent 2013\u00a0study by researchers from the University of Illinois\u00a0that says that honesty leads to more positive feelings among children about illicit drug use.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>But few practitioners, if any, advocate lying.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\u201cIt\u2019s best to react with honesty,\u201d said Tara Lally, a licensed psychologist with a practice in West Long Branch and an adjunct professor at Monmouth University. \u201cIf you lie, you damage your relationship.\u201d</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div height=\"72\"></span><div class=\"vgm-video-title\">\n                        Taking unwanted drugs off the street\n                    </div></li></ul></div></div></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p><strong>Studies differ</strong></p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The issue has produced some conflicting research.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>According to a 2009 survey by the Hazelden Betty Ford Foundation, 63 percent of respondents said that hearing their parents' stories about past alcohol or drug use would make them more responsible about their own use of drugs.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The foundation, based in Minnesota, was formed when Hazelden Treatment Center, a 12-step recovery program center that started in 1949, merged with Betty Ford Center in 2014.\u00a0 It is now one of the largest nonprofit treatment providers in the country with 17 programs in nine states. Their research is frequently cited by academic journals.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>The survey, \"Four Generations Overcoming Addiction,\"\u00a0also reported that 90 percent of teens whose parents told them of past drug or alcohol use as teenagers were likely to consider them to be role models, as opposed to 93 percent of teens who knew nothing about their parents\u2019 past. The point is that disclosure of drug use did little to\u00a0harm the stature of parents in the view of their children and that honesty about past parental drug use is a positive thing in terms of a child's attitudes about illicit drug use, according to the survey.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-PyqKl-3H2rc\" class=\"story-asset oembed-asset\"><div class=\"story-oembed-wrap \"><div \n        class=\"js-oembed story-oembed story-oembed-usa-today story-oembed-type-link\"\n        data-oembed-type=\"link\"\n        data-oembed-provider=\"usa-today\"><style>\n    .oembed-asset-link { border-bottom: 1px solid #e1e1e1; }\n    .oembed-link-anchor { display: block; clear: both; }\n    p.oembed-link-desc { font-size: 100%; color: #666; font-weight: normal; margin: 0 14px 14px 14px; font-family: 'Futura Today Light'; text-align: left; line-height: 120%; }\n</style><div class=\"oembed-asset oembed-asset-link oembed-asset-usa-today oembed-simple-link-container\"><a href=\"http://www.usatoday.com/story/news/nation-now/2017/02/10/heroin-epidemic-overdoses/97745102/\" data-track-label=\"newrecircinline\" class=\"oembed-simple-link oembed-link-anchor\"><p class=\"oembed-link-desc\">1 day, 1 city: 43 overdoses, 1 death</p><div style=\"clear: both;\"></div></a></div></div></div></div><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\u201cWe\u2019re certain that young people want, need and expect their parents to be their No. 1 source for accurate information,\u201d William Moyers, a spokesman for the foundation said.\u00a0\u201cIf not us, who? The internet? Much of that information is dangerous, inaccurate or enticing to young people.\u201d</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>But research published in the social science\u00a0journal Human Communication Research\u00a0in 2013 warned that disclosure could have a bad unintended effect. The study focused on 561 sixth-through-eighth-grade students in Illinois.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>\u201cKnowing that their parents tried substances may actually normalize this behavior for youth,\u201d wrote the study's lead author, Jennifer A. Kam, then of the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign.\u00a0\u00a0\u201cParents\u2019 discussion of their prior use may in some ways downplay the parents\u2019 emphasis on the negative consequences of using substances.\u201d</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Parents\u2019 references to their own drug use \u201cactually may be perceived as permissive,\u201d she\u00a0wrote.</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><!-- cxenseparse_start --><p>Even more disconcerting for truth advocates, the study found that if parents did not disclose their own drug use to their children\u00a0but presented a strong anti-drug message the children were more likely to have anti-drug attitudes. In other words, nondisclosure with warnings about drugs worked. (The survey did not deal with what happens if children\u00a0find\u00a0out if\u00a0those parents had drugs in their past.)</p><!-- cxenseparse_end --><div id=\"module-position-PyqKl-3YLiQ\" "http://rssfeeds.usatoday.com/~/277253806/0/usatoday-newstopstories~Do-you-tell-your-kids-about-your-past-drug-use/", "date": "03-04-2017", "source": "new-york-times", "text": "CLOSE Skip in Skip x Embed x Share Courtesy of Partnership for Drug-Free Kids\n\nParents and guardians face increasing pressure to talk to their kids about drugs. (Photo: alfexe, Getty Images/iStockphoto)\n\nAs the threat of the opioid epidemic reaches more families, parents and guardians face increasing pressure to talk to their kids about drugs, public health officials say.\n\nHow they respond matters, according to a body of research from academic journals and nonprofit organizations. But that research differs on how to respond. And not everyone agrees that honesty is the only policy.\n\nSome psychologists stress caution.\n\nAngelo Valente, executive director of Partnership for a Drug-Free New Jersey, a drug prevention organization, said the question should be viewed as an opportunity.\n\n\"The fact that your child is coming to you is extremely important,\" he said, siding strongly with parents being honest. If dishonesty comes to light, it can harm trust and that can keep a child from coming forward later, he said.\n\n\"You want them to continue to be able to build trust,\" he said. \"You want the parent to be a resource during difficult times in adolescence.\"\n\nAlong with any disclosure of past drug use, parents should make sure their children know that drugs \u2014 from opioids to synthetic drugs like bath salts \u2014 are far more dangerous than most drugs were 20 or 30 years ago, Valente said.\n\nThe national organization, Partnership for Drug-Free Kids, provides advice on how to answer the question in a tip sheet and a more detailed kit specifically about marijuana.\n\n\"Kids want to have that open dialogue,\" said Kristi Rowe, a spokeswoman for the group. \"That doesn't mean you have to give them all the detail in technicolor.\"\n\nOne of the group's broader points: the right approach depends on your child.\n\nTelling the truth, for instance, carries its own pitfalls, according to one prominent 2013 study by researchers from the University of Illinois that says that honesty leads to more positive feelings among children about illicit drug use.\n\nBut few practitioners, if any, advocate lying.\n\n\u201cIt\u2019s best to react with honesty,\u201d said Tara Lally, a licensed psychologist with a practice in West Long Branch and an adjunct professor at Monmouth University. \u201cIf you lie, you damage your relationship.\u201d\n\nCLOSE Skip in Skip x Embed x Share WATCH: FIGHTING HEROIN AND ADDICTION AT THE SHORE WATCH: Children of Heroin | 13:35 Heroin destroys thousands of lives everyday around the world. Staff video by Tom Spader 24 of 24 Last VideoNext Video WATCH: Children of Heroin\n\nWATCH: Carly Boldman talks about her father's struggle with heroin\n\nWATCH: Addicted mothers share their stories of recovery\n\nWATCH: Kim Boldman speaks about her husband's heroin addiction\n\nWATCH: John Brogan talk about his experience with heroin addiction\n\nGetting a job is a hurdle for recovering addicts\n\nChristie details a story of addiction and recovery during State of the State\n\nSon's death spurs Narcan training\n\nHeroin's deadly companion\n\nAdvocates: more drug treatment beds needed\n\nWATCH: Christie salutes those who helped save lives with Narcan\n\nWATCH: Former addicted mother talks being away from children\n\nWATCH: Mothers addicted to heroin share their stories, Part 2\n\nChildren of Heroin: Isaac Edelhauser\n\nBerkeley heroin factory seized\n\nChristie joins opioid addiction panel\n\nChristie on New Jersey's heroin recovery coach program\n\nChristie recognizes recovery coach from Toms River\n\nWATCH: I'm addicted to heroin\n\nWATCH: Former addicted mother shares her experience\n\nThe Warning Signs\n\nA father's fight against heroin in Lacey\n\nDoctor arrested for dealing prescription drugs\n\nTaking unwanted drugs off the street\n\nStudies differ\n\nThe issue has produced some conflicting research.\n\nAccording to a 2009 survey by the Hazelden Betty Ford Foundation, 63 percent of respondents said that hearing their parents' stories about past alcohol or drug use would make them more responsible about their own use of drugs.\n\nThe foundation, based in Minnesota, was formed when Hazelden Treatment Center, a 12-step recovery program center that started in 1949, merged with Betty Ford Center in 2014. It is now one of the largest nonprofit treatment providers in the country with 17 programs in nine states. Their research is frequently cited by academic journals.\n\nThe survey, \"Four Generations Overcoming Addiction,\" also reported that 90 percent of teens whose parents told them of past drug or alcohol use as teenagers were likely to consider them to be role models, as opposed to 93 percent of teens who knew nothing about their parents\u2019 past. The point is that disclosure of drug use did little to harm the stature of parents in the view of their children and that honesty about past parental drug use is a positive thing in terms of a child's attitudes about illicit drug use, according to the survey.\n\n\u201cWe\u2019re certain that young people want, need and expect their parents to be their No. 1 source for accurate information,\u201d William Moyers, a spokesman for the foundation said. \u201cIf not us, who? The internet? Much of that information is dangerous, inaccurate or enticing to young people.\u201d\n\nBut research published in the social science journal Human Communication Research in 2013 warned that disclosure could have a bad unintended effect. The study focused on 561 sixth-through-eighth-grade students in Illinois.\n\n\u201cKnowing that their parents tried substances may actually normalize this behavior for youth,\u201d wrote the study's lead author, Jennifer A. Kam, then of the University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign. \u201cParents\u2019 discussion of their prior use may in some ways downplay the parents\u2019 emphasis on the negative consequences of using substances.\u201d\n\nParents\u2019 references to their own drug use \u201cactually may be perceived as permissive,\u201d she wrote.\n\nEven more disconcerting for truth advocates, the study found that if parents did not disclose their own drug use to their children but presented a strong anti-drug message the children were more likely to have anti-drug attitudes. In other words, nondisclosure with warnings about drugs worked. (The survey did not deal with what happens if children find out if those parents had drugs in their past.)\n\nCLOSE Skip in Skip x Embed x Share Justine Frank of Hamilton discusses her regrets about not disclosing her drug use to her son Jon, who is struggling with addiction Ken Serrano\n\nWhat you say\n\nIf a parent discloses past drug use to a child, regret, bad judgment and the dangers of drugs are the correct things to focus on, Lally said.\n\nThat discussion changes depending on the age of the child. If a 7-year-old asks, it's more important to find out why they are asking, although lying would still be unwise, Lally said.\n\nA 17-year-old needs a more open discussion, she said.\n\nLally said the drug talk with children should not be just one-and-done.\n\n\u201cYou want to make it more of a fluent, casual conversation over time,\u201d she said. \u201cParents are so fearful of glorifying. We don\u2019t discuss family addiction.\u201d\n\nAnd parents should realize \u201cit\u2019s awkward,\u201d she said.\n\nBut those parents boasting of their exploits with drugs or merely focused on the fun of doing drugs can urge their kids to follow their lead without meaning to. Children view disclosures like that as a legacy they must uphold, Lally said.\n\n\u201cThey not only emulate it but surpass it,\u201d she said.\n\nContact Ken Serrano at kserrano@gannettnj.com\n\nRead
